1
PEW RESEARCH CENTER

Appendix E: Results by country
This appendix shows the results for each of the 198 countries and territories included in this study
on the 20 questions that comprise Pew Research Center’s Government Restrictions Index (GRI),
and on the 13 questions that comprise the Center’s Social Hostilities Index (SHI).
Although each question is weighted equally, many of the questions contain gradations, allowing
partial scores depending on intensity or severity. For example, on SHI Question No. 2 – “Was
there mob violence related to religion?” – a country that had no religion-related mob violence
would receive 0.00 points, a country that had mob violence in which no deaths were reported
would receive half of a point (0.50), and a country that had mob violence in which deaths were
reported would receive a full point (1.00).
The scores are presented for three years: the latest year, ending Dec. 31, 2023; the previous year,
ending Dec. 31, 2022; and the baseline year, ending in mid-2007 (July 1, 2006-June 30, 2007).
Scores for other years are not presented due to space constraints.
Both the GRI and the SHI are scales of 0 to 10. Readers wishing to independently calculate a
country’s index score from the tables should note that the sum of a country’s scores on all 20 GRI
questions must be divided by two and the sum of its scores on all 13 SHI questions must be
multiplied by 10/13 (0.77) to convert them to the 10-point scale. For more detail on the indexes,
refer to the Methodology.
Starting with 2017 data, the calculation for SHI Question No. 1 – “Were there crimes, malicious
acts or violence motivated by religious hatred or bias?” – was updated. Read the Methodology for
details.
South Sudan became an independent state in 2011 and does not have any data for 2007 (it is
denoted with “n/a” for 2007 data in this appendix).
Myanmar is also called Burma. Eswatini was formerly known as Swaziland. For more information
on country name changes, refer to the Methodology.

www.pewresearch.org

GRI.Q.1
Does the constitution, or law that functions in the place of a constitution (basic law), specifically provide for "freedom of religion" or include language used in Article 18 of the United
1
Nations Universal Declaration of Human Rights?

0 point 0.00 Yes
- 1/2 point 0.50 The constitution or basic law does not specifically provide for freedom of religion but does protect some religious practices
- Full point 1.00 No

1

Article 18 states: "Everyone has the right to freedom of thought, conscience and religion; this right includes freedom to change his religion or belief, and freedom, either alone or in community with others and in public or private, to manifest his
religion or belief in teaching, practice, worship and observance."
Note: This report corrects the way constitutions were coded for 10 countries: Cameroon, Costa Rica, the Dominican Republic, Fiji, Iran, Jamaica, Kenya, Mexico, Mozambique and Uruguay. The corrections were applied to all applicable previous years
to ensure consistency, and the updates resulted in changes to some of those countries’ GRI.Q.1 scores in various years.

www.pewforum.org

2

GRI.Q.2
Does the constitution or basic law include stipulations that appear to qualify or substantially contradict the concept of “religious freedom”?

- 0 point 0.00 No
- 1/3 point 0.33 Yes, there is a qualification
- 2/3 point 0.67 Yes, there is a substantial contradiction and only some religious practices are protected
- Full point 1.00 Religious freedom is not provided in the first place

3

GRI.Q.3
Taken together, how do the constitution/basic law and other national laws and policies affect religious freedom?
0 point 0.00 National laws and policies provide for religious freedom, and the national government respects religious freedom in practice
- 1/3 point 0.33 National laws and policies provide for religious freedom, and the national government generally respects religious freedom in practice; but there are some instances
(e.g., in certain localities) where religious freedom is not respected in practice
- 2/3 point 0.67 There are limited national legal protections for religious freedom, but the national government does not generally respect religious freedom in practice
- Full point 1.00 National laws and policies do not provide for religious freedom and the national government does not respect religious freedom in practice

Does any level of government interfere with worship or other religious practices?

0 point 0.00 No

- Full point 1.00 Government prohibits worship or religious practices of one or more religious groups as a general policy

GRI.Q.19
Did any level of government use force toward religious groups that resulted in individuals being killed, physically abused, imprisoned, detained or displaced from their homes, or
having their personal or religious properties damaged or destroyed?
0 point 0.00 No
- 1/5 point 0.20 Yes, 1-9 case(s) of government force
- 2/5 point 0.40 Yes, 10-200 cases of government force
- 3/5 point 0.60 Yes, 201-1,000 cases of government force
- 4/5 point 0.80 Yes, 1,001-9,999 cases of government force
- Full point 1.00 Yes, 10,000+ cases of government force

GRI.Q.20
Do some religious groups receive government support or favors, such as funding, official recognition or special access?
For some of the components below, coders may assign partial values
0 point 0.00 No
+ 1/5 point 0.20 Yes, the country's constitution or basic law recognizes a favored religion or religions (refer to GRI.Q.20.1)
+ 1/5 point 0.20 Yes, one or more religious groups have privileges or government access unavailable to other religious groups (refer to GRI.Q.20.2)
+ 1/5 point 0.20 Yes, the government provides funds or other resources to one or more religious groups (refer to GRI.Q.20.3)
+ 1/5 point 0.20 Yes, religious education is required in public schools by local governments or the national government (refer to GRI.Q.20.4)

22

GRI.Q.20.2
Do all religious groups receive the same level of government access and privileges?
- 0 point 0.00 All religious groups are generally treated the same
- 1/4 point 0.25 Some religious groups have minimal privileges unavailable to other religious groups, limited to things such as inheriting buildings or properties
- 2/4 point 0.50 Some religious groups have general privileges or government access unavailable to other religious groups
- 3/4 point 0.75 One religious group has privileges or government access unavailable to other religious groups, but it is not recognized as the country's official religion
- Full point 1.00 One religious group has privileges or government access unavailable to other religious groups, and it is recognized by the national government as the official religion

Note: This is a summary variable that captures the hostilities identified in questions SHI.Q.1a-f into a single measure indicating the severity of religious hatred or bias in each country. Countries can have multiple types of hostilities. A country receives
1/6 point for each type of hostility that occurred that year. For SHI.Q.1a, starting in coding year 2017, the country could receive 0.5/6 point or 1/6 point. The scores on this question are cumulative; a country with two types of malicious acts could score
0.33 for that period and a country with all six types in a year could score 1.00. Starting with 2017 data, there has been an update in calculating SHI.Q.1 to account for limited (0.5 point) and widespread (1.00 point) harassment or intimidation (in
SHI.Q.1a). Prior to 2017, SHI.Q.1a only received 0.00 or 1.00 point. Read the Methodology for more information.

www.pewforum.org

38

SHI.Q.4
Were religion-related terrorist groups active in the country?
- 0 point 0.00 No
- 1/4 point 0.25 Yes, but their activity was limited to recruitment or fundraising
- 2/4 point 0.50 Yes, with violence that resulted in some casualties (1-9 injuries or deaths)
- 3/4 point 0.75 Yes, with violence that resulted in multiple casualties (10-50 injuries or deaths)
- Full point 1.00 Yes, with violence that resulted in many casualties (more than 50 injuries or deaths)

Was there a religion-related war or armed conflict in the country (including ongoing displacements from previous wars)?
- 0 point 0.00 No
- 1/4 point 0.25 Yes, with fewer than 10,000 casualties or people displaced
- 2/4 point 0.50 Yes, with tens of thousands of casualties or people displaced
- 3/4 point 0.75 Yes, with hundreds of thousands of casualties or people displaced
- Full point 1.00 Yes, with millions of casualties or people displaced

SHI.Q.6
Did violence result from tensions between religious groups?

- 0 point 0.00 No
- 1/3 point 0.33 There were public tensions between religious groups, but they fell short of hostilities involving physical violence
- 2/3 point 0.67 Yes, with physical violence in a few cases
- Full point 1.00 Yes, with physical violence in numerous cases
